<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41597"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome sequence of an upland wild rice species, Oryza granulata"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-29"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Exploiting novel gene sources from wild relatives has proven to be an efficient approach to advance crop genetic breeding efforts. Oryza granulata, with the GG genome type, occupies the basal position of the Oryza phylogeny and has the second largest genome (~882 Mb). As an upland wild rice species, it possesses renowned traits that distinguish it from other Oryza species, such as tolerance to shade and drought, immunity to bacterial blight and resistance to the brown planthopper. Here, we generated a 736.66-Mb genome assembly of O. granulata with 40,131 predicted protein-coding genes. With Hi-C data, for the first time, we anchored ~98.2% of the genome assembly to the twelve pseudo-chromosomes. This chromosome-length genome assembly of O. granulata will provide novel insights into rice genome evolution, enhance our efforts to search for new genes for future rice breeding programmes and facilitate the conservation of germplasm of this endangered wild rice species. 
                
                  
                    
                    
                    
                  
                
               Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12063198"/>

        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Exploiting novel gene sources from wild relatives has proven to be an efficient approach to advance crop genetic breeding efforts. <i>Oryza granulata</i>, with the GG genome type, occupies the basal position of the <i>Oryza</i> phylogeny and has the second largest genome (~882 Mb). As an upland wild rice species, it possesses renowned traits that distinguish it from other <i>Oryza</i> species, such as tolerance to shade and drought, immunity to bacterial blight and resistance to the brown planthopper. Here, we generated a 736.66-Mb genome assembly of <i>O. granulata</i> with 40,131 predicted protein-coding genes. With Hi-C data, for the first time, we anchored ~98.2% of the genome assembly to the twelve pseudo-chromosomes. This chromosome-length genome assembly of <i>O. granulata</i> will provide novel insights into rice genome evolution, enhance our efforts to search for new genes for future rice breeding programmes and facilitate the conservation of germplasm of this endangered wild rice species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Measurement(s)
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          DNA • RNA • transcriptome • genome coverage • sequence_assembly • sequence feature annotation
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Technology Type(s)
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          DNA sequencing • RNA sequencing • flow cytometry method • computational modeling technique • sequence assembly process • sequence annotation
                        </td></tr><tr><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Sample Characteristic - Organism
                        </td><td class="u-text-left ">
                          Oryza granulata
                        </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>
              <p>Machine-accessible metadata file describing the reported data: <a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12063198">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12063198</a></p></div></div></section>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>As one of the most important crops in the world, rice is the most water-consuming cereal. Rice cultivation and yield depend greatly on water resources. The genetic breeding of drought-tolerant rice is a promising direction under the currently mounting water shortage. However, most species in the genus <i>Oryza</i> prefer moist and even aquatic habitats, and thus, upland rice breeding is very demanding due to the scarcity of gene sources with drought tolerance in the genus <i>Oryza</i>.</p><p>The genus <i>Oryza</i> contains more than twenty species, including two cultivated species domesticated independently from different wild species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4230e514">1</a></sup>. Compared to the majority of other grass species, <i>Oryza</i> species have relatively small genomes and abundant morphological and ecological diversity. <i>Oryza granulata</i> occupies the basal position of the <i>Oryza</i> phylogeny, first diverging from other members of the genus 8.8–10.2 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Guo, Y. L. &amp; Ge, S. Advances in the study of systematics and evolution of the tribe Oryzeae (Poaceae). Acta Phytotaxon. Sin. 44, 211–230 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4230e528">2</a></sup>. <i>O. exasperata</i> (A. Braun) Heer was identified according to a spikelet fossil, which was found in an excavation site of Miocene age in Germany and appears to resemble the spikelet of extant <i>O. granulata</i> based on its morphology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Heer, O. Flora Tertiaria Helvetiae - Die tertiäre Flora der Schweiz. (J. Würster &amp; Compagnie, 1855)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4230e538">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Tang, L. et al. Phylogeny and biogeography of the rice tribe (Oryzeae): evidence from combined analysis of 20 chloroplast fragments. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 54, 266–277 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4230e541">4</a></sup>. As an upland wild rice species, <i>O. granulata</i> possesses renowned traits that distinguish it from other <i>Oryza</i> species, such as tolerance to shade and drought, immunity to bacterial blight and resistance to the brown planthopper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Department of Agronomy, Kwangtung Agrieultural and Forestry College. The species of wild rice and their geographical distribution in China. J. Genet. Genomics 2, 31–36 (1975)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4230e552">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The Cooperative Team of Wild Rice Resources Survey and Exploration of China. A general survey and exploration of wild rice germplasm resources in China. Sci. Agric. Sinica 17, 27–34 (1984)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4230e552_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fan, S. G., Zhang, Z. J., Liu, L., Liu, H. X. &amp; Liang, C. Y. The species, geographical distribution of wild rice and their characteristics in China. J. Wuhan Bot. Res. 18, 417–425 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4230e555">7</a></sup>. Because of the distant evolutionary relationship of this species with cultivated rice, it has long been challenging to apply conventional methods used in rice breeding programmes to it.</p><p>Compared to that for other wild species closely related to cultivated rice, little effort has been made to perform genetic studies and germplasm exploitation in <i>O. granulata</i> due to the lack of a high-quality genome assembly. Among the diploid <i>Oryza</i> species, <i>O. granulata</i> (GG genome type) has the second largest genome (~882 Mb), smaller than only that of <i>O. australiensis</i> (~965 Mb, EE genome type)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4230e574">8</a></sup>, which is approximately two times larger than the rice genome (~420 Mb, AA genome type)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92–100 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4230e579">9</a></sup>. The two-fold increase in genome size is mainly due to the accumulation of transposable elements (TEs) in <i>O. granulata</i>, which may have seriously eroded genome collinearity compared with that in other related rice species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ammiraju, J. S. S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4230e586">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: Dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4230e589">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wu, Z. G. et al. De novo genome assembly of Oryza granulata reveals rapid genome expansion and adaptive evolution. Commun. Biol. 1, 84 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4230e592">11</a></sup>. In the last decade, great progress has been made in comparative genomics of cultivated rice and its wild relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, M. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4230e596">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4230e599">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat. Commun. 4, 1595 (2013)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4230e599_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, W. et al. Improved hybrid de novo genome assembly and annotation of African wild rice, Oryza longistaminata, from Illumina and PacBio sequencing reads. Plant Genome-US, e20001 (2020)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4230e599_2">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, W. et al. SMRT sequencing of the Oryza rufipogon genome reveals the genomic basis of rice adaptation. Commun. Biol. 3, 167 (2020)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4230e599_3">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stein, J. C. et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat. Genet. 50, 285–296 (2018)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4230e599_4">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, Q.-J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4230e602">17</a></sup>, with much of this work performed at the chromosome scale. In the first released genome assembly of <i>O. granulata</i>, the assembled genome sequences were not anchored to the chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wu, Z. G. et al. De novo genome assembly of Oryza granulata reveals rapid genome expansion and adaptive evolution. Commun. Biol. 1, 84 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4230e609">11</a></sup>. This undoubtedly limits the use of <i>O. granulata</i> as a basic <i>Oryza</i> lineage to accurately infer the genome evolution of <i>O. granulata</i> compared to other rice species at the chromosome level.</p><p><i>O. granulata</i> is naturally distributed in South Asia, including China, India, Cambodia, Indonesia, Laos, Myanmar, Nepal, the Philippines, Sri Lanka, and Thailand<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vaughan, D. A. The Wild Relatives of Rice: A Genetic Resources Handbook. (IRRI, 1994)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4230e628">18</a></sup>. It is seriously threatened due to ongoing human disturbance and rapid deforestation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gao, L. Z., Zhang, S. Z., Zhou, Y., Ge, S. &amp; Hong, D. Y. A survey of the current status of wild rice in China. Biodiv. Sci 4(3), 160–166 (1996)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4230e632">19</a></sup>. Previous population genetic studies revealed that this species possesses fairly low levels of genetic diversity within populations but high genetic differentiation among populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Gao, L. Z., Ge, S. &amp; Hong, D. Y. Low levels of genetic diversity within populations and high differentiation among populations of a wild rice, Oryza granulata Nees et. Arn. ex. Watt. from China. Int. J. Plant Sci. 161, 691–697 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4230e636">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Gao, L. Z. et al. Studies on population genetic structure of Oryza granulata Nees et. Arn. ex. Watt. from Yunnan and its in situ conservation significance. Sci. China Ser. C, 297–302 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4230e639">21</a></sup>. The considerable genomic diversity detected through pan-genome analysis demonstrates that <i>de novo</i> assembly of more than one genome helps reveal the origin and evolutionary forces of population structure and levels of genomic diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4230e646">22</a></sup>. Thus, sequencing an additional genome of <i>O. granulata</i> from a genetically different population compared with the previously sequenced accession collected in India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wu, Z. G. et al. De novo genome assembly of Oryza granulata reveals rapid genome expansion and adaptive evolution. Commun. Biol. 1, 84 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4230e654">11</a></sup> is needed.</p><p>The availability of a chromosome-scale genome of <i>O. granulata</i> will lay the foundation for further evolutionary studies as well as the improvement of desired agronomic traits relevant to rice breeding programmes. Here, we present a new chromosome-scale genome of <i>O. granulata</i> assembled <i>de novo</i> using the Illumina and Hi-C sequencing platforms. In contrast to the previously sequenced <i>O. granulata</i> accession (IRGC Acc. No. 102117) from India, the sequenced plant was collected in Yunnan, China, and thus, the plants were geographically separated. The obtained genome assembly will provide novel insights into the genomic diversity and genome evolution of the genus <i>Oryza</i> and enhance the exploration of precious wild rice germplasm resources.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant material collection, total DNA isolation and genome sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected dozens of <i>O. granulata</i> plants from Menghai County, Yunnan Province, China, which were planted in the greenhouse of the Kunming Institute of Botany, Chinese Academy of Sciences. Fresh and healthy leaves were harvested from the best-growing individual and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen, followed by preservation at −80 °C in the laboratory prior to DNA extraction. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from leaves using a modified CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Porebski, S., Bailey, L. G. &amp; Baum, B. R. Modification of a CTAB DNA extraction protocol for plants containing high polysaccharide and polyphenol components. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 15, 8–15 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4230e692">23</a></sup>. RNase A was used to remove RNA contaminants. The quality and quantity of the extracted DNA were examined using a NanoDrop 2000 spectrophotometer (NanoDrop Technologies, Wilmington, DE, USA) and electrophoresis on a 0.8% agarose gel, respectively.</p><p>A total of three 260-bp short-insert libraries and five long-insert libraries (3 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb) were prepared following Illumina’s instructions. Then, the Illumina HiSeq. 2000 (PE100 and PE101) and HiSeq. 2500 (PE125 and PE150) platforms were employed for whole-genome sequencing according to the standard Illumina protocols (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA). In total, we generated approximately 133.38 Gb (~168.41×) of raw data (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Libraries and read statistics used for the <i>O. granulata</i> genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Hi-C data preparation</h3><p>We constructed Hi-C libraries using young leaves collected from the same individual plant of <i>O. granulata</i> for high-quality DNA isolation by following the standard protocol described previously with certain modifications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268–276 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4230e851">24</a></sup>. Approximately 5-g leaf samples were cut into minute pieces and cross-linked by 2% formaldehyde solution at room temperature for 15 minutes. Then, the sample was mixed with excess 2.5 M glycine to stop the cross-linking reaction and neutralize the remaining formaldehyde. The Hi-C library was constructed and sequenced by Annoroad Genomics (Beijing, China) with the standard procedure described as follows. The cross-linked DNA was extracted and then digested with <i>Mbo</i>I restriction enzyme. The sticky ends of the digested fragments were biotinylated and proximity ligated to form ligation junctions that were enriched for and then ultrasonically sheared to a size of 200–500 bp. The biotin-labelled DNA fragments were pulled down and ligated with Illumina paired-end adapters and then amplified by PCR to produce the Hi-C sequencing library. The library was sequenced with the Illumina HiSeq. X Ten (PE150) platform, and a total of ~109.41 Gb (~138.15×) of raw sequencing data was produced (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">RNA isolation and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>A total of seven tissues representing different developmental stages of <i>O. granulata</i> were sampled to generate the RNA-Seq data needed for subsequent genome annotation. These tissues included panicles at three different stages of flower development, flag leaves, and stems and the shoots and roots of three-leaf seedlings. Because of the low germination rate of <i>O. granulata</i>, seedlings were germinated from seeds harvested from multiple plants, while the remaining tissues were sampled from the individual used for genome sequencing. All collected samples were quickly frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored in a refrigerator at −80 °C before RNA extraction.</p><p>RNA was individually extracted from each tissue using TRI reagent (Molecular Research Centre, Inc., Cincinnati, OH, USA), according to the instructions provided with the reagents. Seven libraries were constructed and sequenced by Biomarker Technologies (Beijing, China) on the Illumina HiSeq. 2500 platform with a read length of 126 bp. In total, ~21.8 Gb of high-quality data was obtained and used for subsequent assembly after filtering the low-quality and duplicated reads caused by PCR amplification (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Clean RNA-Seq data of <i>O. granulata</i> from seven tissues.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Estimation of genome size</h3><p>The genome size of <i>O. granulata</i> was estimated using two methods, including <i>k</i>-mer frequency distribution and flow cytometric analysis. We first estimated and validated the genome size of <i>O. granulata</i> using flow cytometric analysis. A total of 40-50 mg of fresh leaves was collected for sample preparation using the OTTO method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Loureiro, J., Rodriguez, E., Dolezel, J. &amp; Santos, C. Comparison of four nuclear isolation buffers for plant DNA flow cytometry. Ann. Bot.-London 98, 679–689 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4230e994">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Huang, H., Tong, Y., Zhang, Q. J. &amp; Gao, L. Z. Genome size variation among and within Camellia species by using flow cytometric analysis. Plos One 8, e64981 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4230e997">26</a></sup>. Nuclear samples were analysed using a BD FACSCalibur (BD Biosciences, USA) flow cytometer. CellQuest software (BD Biosciences, USA) was used to analyse the flow cytometry results and gate all cells of interest. Here, CV = D/M × 100%, where D is the standard deviation of the cell distribution and M is the average of the cell distribution. The average coefficient of variation (CV) was used to evaluate the results, with CV &lt; 5% considered reliable. Nuclear DNA content was calculated as a linear relationship between the ratios of 2C-value peaks of the sample and standard.</p><p>When <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare (~389 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Goff, S. A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92–100 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1010">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1013">12</a></sup> and <i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>mays</i> var. B73 (2,300 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1024">27</a></sup> were employed as inner standards, the estimated genome size of <i>O. granulata</i> was ~672 Mb and ~707 Mb, respectively, both of which were smaller than the previous estimate (882 Mb) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Fig1">1</a>). Meanwhile, we generated the 17-mer occurrence distribution of sequencing reads from short libraries using the <i>k</i>-mer method (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Fig2">2</a>). Then, we estimated the genome size to be ~792 Mb, and the proportion of repeat sequences and heterozygosity rate of the genome were determined to be approximately 70.7% and 0.76%, respectively, using GCE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Liu, B. H. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Preprint at, &#xA;http://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012v1&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1040">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="332" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Cytogram of the fluorescence intensity of <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare, <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>Z. mays</i> ssp. <i>mays</i> var. B73 nuclei isolated with Otto’s buffer. All CV values were &lt;5%.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="611" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The 17-mer distribution of sequencing reads from <i>O. granulata</i>. The occurrence of 17-mers was calculated using GCE based on the sequencing data from short-insert-size libraries (insert size ≤500 bp) of <i>O. granulata</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome assembly</h3><p>We assembled the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1094">29</a></sup> and SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1098">30</a></sup>. First, the high-quality paired-end Illumina reads from short-insert-size libraries were assembled into contig sequences using ALLPATHS-LG. This process yielded assembly results with a contig N50 value of 22,359 bp and total length of ~732.33 Mb. Second, all mate-pair reads with large insert sizes (≥2 kb) were aligned onto the preassembled contigs. According to the order and distance information, the assembled contigs were further elongated and eventually combined into scaffolds using SSPACE. We closed the gaps that might be repeat sequences masked during the construction of scaffolds using GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Luo, R. B. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1102">31</a></sup>. Briefly, all paired-end sequencing reads were first mapped onto the assembled scaffolds, and then those read pairs with one read well aligned to the contigs and another located in a gap region were retrieved and locally assembled to close gaps. Consequently, the <i>O. granulata</i> genome assembly had a total length of ~736.66 Mb, which accounted for ~93% of the genome size estimated by <i>k</i>-mer analysis, containing 2,393 scaffolds (N50 = 916.3 kb; N90 = 239.8 kb) and 29,963 contigs (N50 = 43.9 kb; N90 = 12.0 kb). There were 1,146 scaffolds with lengths &gt;100 kb, among which the largest scaffold had a sequence length of 4,040,447 bp (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Scaffold length distribution of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Three approaches were used to evaluate the completeness and accuracy of this genome assembly. First, we mapped all high-quality reads (~186.8 million, ~87×) from short-insert-size libraries back to the assembly using BWA (Burrows-Wheeler Aligner)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at, &#xA;http://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997v2&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1246">32</a></sup>, showing good alignments with an average mapping rate of ~99.46%. Second, the completeness of genome assembly and gene prediction was assessed with BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1250">33</a></sup> according to collections from the Embryophyta lineage. Our gene predictions revealed 1,390 (96.53%) of the 1,440 highly conserved core proteins in the Embryophyta lineage. Third, the RNA sequencing reads generated in this study were assembled into a total of 137,380 transcripts using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1254">34</a></sup>, which had an N50 value of 1,035 bp and a total length of ~88.8 Mb. Then, they were aligned back to the genome assembly using GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1258">35</a></sup>. Our results showed that a total of 89,977 transcripts could be successfully aligned to the genome assembly with a mapping rate of 65.5%. After filtering the low-quality reads using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bolger, A. M., Usadel, B. &amp; Lohse, M. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1262">36</a></sup>, clean paired-end reads of Hi-C data were mapped to the assembled scaffolds by BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at, &#xA;http://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997v2&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1267">32</a></sup>. Finally, 1,265 (723.2 Mb, 98.2% of the assembled length) of 2,393 scaffolds were mapped, grouped and ordered into 12 chromosomes using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1271">37</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Assembly statistics of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome sequence.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Annotation of protein-coding genes</h3><p>We predicted protein-coding genes of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using three methods, including <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction, homology-based gene prediction and RNA-Seq-aided gene prediction. Prior to gene prediction, the assembled <i>O. granulata</i> genome was hard and soft masked using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 4, Unit 4.10. (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1416">38</a></sup>. We adopted Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res 32, W309–312 (2004)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1420">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1420_1">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1423">41</a></sup> and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1428">42</a></sup> to perform <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction. Models used for each gene predictor were trained from a set of high-quality proteins generated from the RNA-Seq dataset. We used Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1435">43</a></sup> and GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res 10, 547–548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1439">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1442">45</a></sup> to conduct homology-based gene prediction. First, the protein sequences were aligned to the <i>O. granulata</i> genome assembly using Exonerate with the default parameters. Second, given that GeneWise is a time-consuming program to run, we mapped the protein sequences from <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare (MSU 7.0) to the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using GenBlastA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="She, R., Chu, J. S. C., Wang, K., Pei, J. &amp; Chen, N. S. genBlastA: enabling BLAST to identify homologous gene sequences. Genome Res. 19, 143–149 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1459">46</a></sup> prior to GeneWise prediction. Homologous genomic fragments of the target genes together with their 5-kb upstream and downstream flanking sequences were then extracted using an in-house Perl script. Finally, GeneWise was used to align them against the corresponding proteins to determine gene structures. To carry out RNA-Seq-aided gene prediction, we first assembled clean RNA-Seq reads into transcripts using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1463">34</a></sup>, which were then aligned to our genome assembly using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1467">47</a></sup>. The output included a set of consistent and non-overlapping sequence assemblies, which were used to describe the gene structures.</p><p>We combined all gene structures obtained from the three above-mentioned sets of predictions, including <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions and protein and transcript alignments, with the weighted consensus gene set using EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1477">48</a></sup>. To perform further filtering, the genes with peptide lengths shorter than 50 amino acids and/or containing inner stop codons were removed. In total, 40,131 protein-coding genes with an average length of 3,152 bp were predicted in the assembled <i>O. granulata</i> genome.</p><p>To assess the quality of gene prediction, we compared the length distributions of protein-coding genes, coding sequences (CDS), exons and introns with those from the other four species (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Sorghum bicolor, Z. mays</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>), among which we did not observe any obvious differences in the length distribution of gene features (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Fig3">3</a>; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab5">5</a>). Then, we surveyed the proportion of our predicted <i>O. granulata</i> gene sets supported by RNA-Seq and homologous proteins. We aligned the assembled transcripts against our gene predictions using the BLAST program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1503">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST plus: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1506">50</a></sup>. Only hits with a coverage ≥80% and an identity ≥90% were retained. Our analysis showed that approximately 47.58% (19,094) of the predicted gene models were supported by RNA-Seq data. Next, we downloaded protein sequences of <i>O. sativa</i> ssp. <i>japonica</i> cv. Nipponbare and aligned them to the predicted gene models using BLAST. We filtered those hits with an identity &lt;30% or a gene coverage &lt;80%. We found that 23,871 gene models, accounting for approximately 59.48% of the total genes, were supported by evidence of homologous proteins in rice. Combining genes validated by the two above-described methods, 28,823 genes, representing ~71.82% of the total <i>O. granulata</i> gene set, were supported by RNA-Seq and/or homologous proteins (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab6">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-020-0470-2/MediaObjects/41597_2020_470_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="583" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Comparisons of gene features among <i>O. granulata</i> and the three other species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>). Gene features include gene length, CDS length, exon length and intron length.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-6"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Comparison of gene models among <i>O. granulata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> from Capparidales and the three grasses, namely, rice, maize and sorghum.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/6"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-7"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab6" data-test="table-caption">Table 6 Validation and functional annotation of the <i>O. granulata</i> protein-coding genes.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/7"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Gene functions were inferred according to the best match of the alignments to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Non-Redundant (NR) and Swiss-Prot protein databases using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1784">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST plus: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1787">50</a></sup> and the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database with an E-value threshold of 1E-5. The motifs and domains within gene models were identified by PFAM databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res 36, D281–D288 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1791">51</a></sup>. Gene Ontology (GO) IDs for each gene were obtained from Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Conesa, A. &amp; Gotz, S. Blast2GO: a comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2008, 1–12 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1795">52</a></sup>. In total, approximately 85.81% of the predicted protein-coding genes of <i>O. granulata</i> could be functionally annotated with known genes, conserved domains, and Gene Ontology terms (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab6">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Annotation of non-coding RNA genes</h3><p>Five different types of non-coding RNA genes, namely, transfer RNA (tRNA) genes, ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes, small nucleolar RNA (snoRNA) genes, small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes and microRNA (miRNA) genes, were predicted using <i>de novo</i> and homology search methods. We used tRNAscan-SE algorithms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1816">53</a></sup> with default parameters to identify the genes associated with tRNA, which is an adaptor molecule composed of RNA used in biology to bridge the three-letter genetic code in messenger RNA (mRNA) with the twenty-letter code of amino acids in proteins. The rRNA genes (8S, 18S, and 28S), which are the RNA components of the ribosome and associated with the enzyme representing the site of protein synthesis in all living cells, were predicted using RNAmmer algorithms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res 35, 3100–3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1820">54</a></sup> with default parameters. snoRNAs are a class of small RNA molecules that guide chemical modifications of other RNAs, mainly ribosomal RNAs, transfer RNAs and small nuclear RNAs. The snoRNA genes were annotated using Snoscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. A computational screen for methylation guide snoRNAs in yeast. Science 283, 1168–1171 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1824">55</a></sup> with the yeast rRNA methylation sites and yeast rRNA sequences provided by the Snowscan distribution. snRNA is a class of small RNA molecules that are found within the nucleus of eukaryotic cells. They are involved in a variety of important processes, such as RNA splicing (removal of introns from hnRNA), regulation of transcription factors (7SK RNA) or RNA polymerase II (B2 RNA), and maintenance of telomeres. The snRNA genes were identified by Infernal software against the Rfam database with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 33, D121–D124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1828">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1831">57</a></sup>.</p><p>The miRNA genes were annotated in two steps. First, we downloaded the existing rice miRNA entries from miRBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kozomara, A. &amp; Griffiths-Jones, S. miRBase: integrating microRNA annotation and deep-sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D152–D157 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1838">58</a></sup>. Then, the conserved miRNAs were identified by mapping all miRBase-recorded rice miRNA precursor sequences against the assembled <i>O. granulata</i> genome using BLASTN with cut-offs at an identity &gt;60% and a query coverage &gt;60%. Second, when a miRNA was mapped to the target <i>O. granulata</i> genome, the surrounding sequence was checked for hairpin structures. The loci with miRNA precursor secondary structures were annotated as miRNA genes.</p><p>We annotated a total of 1,003 tRNA genes, 221 rRNA genes, 295 snoRNA genes, 101 snRNA genes and 257 miRNA genes belonging to 50 miRNA families in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab7">7</a>). To investigate miRNA-target genes involved in important biological pathways, the target genes of miRNAs were predicted using the psRNATarget server with default parameters. Finally, 963 miRNA-target sites were identified. The protein sequences of these target genes were blasted against <i>O. sativa</i> proteins in the Rice Genome Annotation Project Database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kawahara, Y. et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice 6, 1–10 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1860">59</a></sup> using the BLASTP program. The results were imported into the agriGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Du, Z., Zhou, X., Ling, Y., Zhang, Z. &amp; Su, Z. agriGO: a GO analysis toolkit for the agricultural community. Nucleic Acids Res 38, W64–W70 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1864">60</a></sup> server by comparing them with the whole set of protein-coding genes of <i>O. sativa</i> as a background. KO (KEGG Orthology) annotation of target genes was implemented using the BlastKOALA program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kanehisa, M., Sato, Y. &amp; Morishima, K. BlastKOALA and GhostKOALA: KEGG tools for functional characterization of genome and metagenome sequences. J. Mol. Biol. 428, 726–731 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1872">61</a></sup> with the eukaryote gene database.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-8"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab7" data-test="table-caption">Table 7 Conserved non-coding RNA genes in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/8"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Annotation of repeat sequences</h3><p>We identified the known TEs within the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using RepeatMasker with the Repbase TE library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Jurka, J. Repbase Update - a database and an electronic journal of repetitive elements. Trends Genet 16, 418–420 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1992">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d4230e1995">63</a></sup>. RepeatProteinMask searches were also conducted using the TE protein database as a query library. The annotation of repeat sequences of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome is summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab8">8</a>. The annotation showed that approximately 61.98% (456.6 Mb) of the assembled genome consisted of repeat sequences, and the proportion of repeat sequences varied largely from one type to another. DNA transposons and other repeats contributed only ~9.83% and ~1.1% to the assembled genome, respectively. In contrast, retrotransposons represented half (~51.05%) of the genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-9"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab8" data-test="table-caption">Table 8 Summary of the annotated repeat sequences in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/9"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We constructed a <i>de novo</i> repeat library of the <i>O. granulata</i> genome using RepeatModeler, which can automatically execute two core <i>de novo</i> repeat-finding programs, namely, RECON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bao, Z. R. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Automated de novo identification of repeat sequence families in sequenced genomes. Genome Res 12, 1269–1276 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2220">64</a></sup> and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, I351–I358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2224">65</a></sup>, to comprehensively conduct, refine and classify consensus models of putative interspersed repeats for the <i>O. granulata</i> genome. Furthermore, we performed a <i>de novo</i> search for long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons against the <i>O. granulata</i> genome sequences using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="McCarthy, E. M. &amp; McDonald, J. F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2238">66</a></sup>. All intact LTR retrotransposons were classified into Ty1/<i>copia</i>, Ty3<i>/gypsy</i> and unclassified groups according to both reverse transcriptase (RT) sequence similarity and the order of ORFs using Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res 36, D281–D288 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2249">51</a></sup>. The RT sequences were retrieved from each retrotransposon element and further checked by homology searches using ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947–2948 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2253">67</a></sup> against the published RTs that were downloaded from the <i>Gypsy</i> Database (GyDB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–D74 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2260">68</a></sup>. LTR retrotransposons (~50.83%) represented most of the RNA transposons in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome, accounting for approximately 43.41% of the assembly. They belonged to two types of LTR retrotransposon superfamilies: Ty1/<i>copia</i> and Ty3/<i>gypsy</i> (~5.58% and ~37.83%, respectively) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab8">8</a>).</p><p>We also identified tandem repeats using the Tandem Repeat Finder (TRF) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2280">69</a></sup> and the non-interspersed repeat sequences, including low-complexity repeats, satellites and simple repeats, using RepeatMasker (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab8">8</a>). A total of six types of simple sequence repeats (SSRs), from mono- to hexa-nucleotides, were identified using the MISA (MIcroSAtellite) identification tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 106, 411–422 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2287">70</a></sup>. The minimum repeat unit size was set at twelve for mono-nucleotides, at six for di-nucleotides, at four for tri-nucleotides, and at three for tetra- to hexa-nucleotides. As a result, a total of 183,339 SSRs were detected in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome. Of these, tri-nucleotide SSRs accounted for the largest proportion, both in quantity and sequence length, followed by tetra-nucleotides, di-nucleotides and other types (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab9">9</a>). These SSRs will provide valuable molecular markers to assist rice breeding programmes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-10"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab9" data-test="table-caption">Table 9 Occurrence of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) in the <i>O. granulata</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2/tables/10"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>All sequencing reads have been deposited into the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP189057&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2414">71</a></sup> and BIG Genome Sequence Archive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="BIGD Genome Sequence Archive, &#xA;http://bigd.big.ac.cn/gsa/browse/CRA001486&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2418">72</a></sup>. The assembled genome sequence is available from the NCBI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="NCBI Assembly, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.gca:GCA_005223365.2&#xA;&#xA; (2020)" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2422">73</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Li, W. et al. Oryza meyeriana var. granulata, whole genome shotgun sequencing project. GenBank, &#xA;https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:SPHZ02000000&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2425">74</a></sup> and BIG Genome Warehouse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="BIGD Genome Warehouse, &#xA;http://bigd.big.ac.cn/search?dbId=gwh%26q=GWHAAKB00000000&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2429">75</a></sup>. The protein-coding gene, non-coding gene, and repeat sequence annotation results and functional prediction results are available from the Figshare database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Shi, C. et al. Annotation results of Oryza granulata genome. figshare, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.8191316&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2433">76</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">RNA integrity</h3><p>Before constructing RNA-Seq libraries, the concentration and amount of total RNA were separately evaluated using a NanoDrop 2000 UV-VIS spectrophotometer (NanoDrop Technologies, Wilmington, DE, USA), and the rRNA ratio and RNA integrity were estimated using an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Palo Alto, CA, USA). For each tissue, only total RNAs with a total amount ≥15 μg, a concentration ≥400 ng/μl, an RNA integrity number (RIN) ≥7, and an rRNA ratio ≥1.4 were used to construct a cDNA library according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Quality filtering of Illumina sequencing raw reads</h3><p>To eliminate adapter contaminants and potential sequencing errors, using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Bolger, A. M., Usadel, B. &amp; Lohse, M. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2457">36</a></sup>, we removed the following five types of reads: (1) reads with ≥10 bp derived from the adapter sequences (allowing ≤10% mismatches); (2) reads with unidentified bases (Ns) constituting ≥10% of their length; (3) reads with ≥40% low-quality bases (Phred score ≤5); (4) reads caused by PCR duplications (i.e., read 1 and read 2 of two paired-end reads that were completely identical); and 5) reads with a <i>k</i>-mer frequency ≤3 (aiming to minimize the influences of sequencing errors). These five filtering processes resulted in a total of ~108.72 Gb (~137.27×) of high-quality data, which were retained and used for subsequent analysis (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Comparisons of the genome assemblies and annotation</h3><p>We produced high-depth sequencing data for <i>O. granulata</i> using the Illumina and Hi-C sequencing platforms. Then, we <i>de novo</i> assembled an ~736.66 Mb genome assembly of <i>O. granulata</i> comprising 2,393 scaffolds with a scaffold N50 of ~916.3 kb (Online-only Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#Tab10">1</a>). The contig N50 was ~43.9 kb, which was higher than that obtained for the genome assemblies of other <i>Oryza</i> species with similar second-generation sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, Q.-J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-020-0470-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4230e2491">17</a></sup>. With Hi-C data, for the first time, we anchored approximately 98.2% of the genome assembly into the twelve pseudo-chromosomes.
